	<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Amtrak</h1>
	
	<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
		<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Amtrak_(disambiguation)" title="Amtrak (disambiguation)">Amtrak (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Intercity rail operator in the United States</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>National Railroad Passenger Corporation (Amtrak)</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_logo.svg"><img alt="Amtrak logo.svg" data-file-height="55" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Amtrak_logo.svg/230px-Amtrak_logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Amtrak_logo.svg/345px-Amtrak_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Amtrak_logo.svg/460px-Amtrak_logo.svg.png 2x" width="230"/></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_network_map_2016.png"><img alt="Amtrak network map 2016.png" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="4800" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Amtrak_network_map_2016.png/250px-Amtrak_network_map_2016.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Amtrak_network_map_2016.png/375px-Amtrak_network_map_2016.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Amtrak_network_map_2016.png/500px-Amtrak_network_map_2016.png 2x" width="250"/></a><div>Geographic map of the Amtrak system <span class="nowrap">(<a class="extiw" href="//sharemap.org/special/North_America_Passenger_Trains" title="sharemap:special/North America Passenger Trains">interactive map</a>)</span></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Reporting_mark" title="Reporting mark">Reporting mark</a></th><td>AMTK, AMTZ (IATA Code : 2V)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Locale</th><td><div class="plainlist">

</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Dates of operation</th><td>May 1, 1971<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winner_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-winner-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-last_court_test_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-last_court_test-2">[2]</a></sup>–present<br/>48 years ago</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Predecessor</th><td><a href="/wiki/List_of_railroads_eligible_to_participate_in_the_formation_of_Amtrak#Participating_railroads" title="List of railroads eligible to participate in the formation of Amtrak">20 privately operated intercity passenger rail systems</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Track_gauge" title="Track gauge">Track gauge</a></th><td><span class="nowrap">4 ft <span class="frac">8<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> in</span> (<span class="nowrap">1,435 mm</span>) <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_gauge" title="Standard gauge">standard gauge</a></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_system" title="Railway electrification system">Electrification</a></th><td><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Amtrak%27s_25_Hz_traction_power_system" title="Amtrak's 25 Hz traction power system"><span class="nowrap">12 kV, 25 Hz AC</span></a>:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> <span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> – <a href="/wiki/New_Rochelle,_New_York" title="New Rochelle, New York">New Rochelle</a>)</span></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_to_Harrisburg_Main_Line" title="Philadelphia to Harrisburg Main Line">Philadelphia to Harrisburg Main Line</a> (<a href="/wiki/Keystone_Corridor" title="Keystone Corridor">Keystone Corridor</a>)</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Amtrak%27s_60_Hz_traction_power_system" title="Amtrak's 60 Hz traction power system"><span class="nowrap">25 kV, 60 Hz AC</span></a>:
<ul><li>Northeast Corridor (<a href="/wiki/New_Rochelle,_New_York" title="New Rochelle, New York">New Rochelle</a> – Boston)</li></ul></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Length</th><td>44 routes (21,400 miles (34,400 km) route miles)<br/>Track owned: 623 miles (1,003 km)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Headquarters</th><td><span class="nowrap">1 Massachusetts Ave, N.W., Washington, D.C.</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Website</th><td><span class="url"><a class="external text" href="https://www.amtrak.com" rel="nofollow">www<wbr/>.amtrak<wbr/>.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>National Railroad Passenger Corporation</b>, <a href="/wiki/Trade_name" title="Trade name">doing business as</a> <b>Amtrak</b>, is a passenger <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport#Passenger_trains" title="Rail transport">railroad</a> service that provides medium- and long-distance <a href="/wiki/Inter-city_rail" title="Inter-city rail">intercity</a> service in the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">contiguous</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and to nine <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canadian</a> cities.
</p><p>Founded in 1971 as a <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprise" title="State-owned enterprise">quasi-public corporation</a> to operate many U.S. passenger rail <span class="nowrap">services,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winner_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-winner-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-last_court_test_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-last_court_test-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amirrim_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-amirrim-3">[3]</a></sup></span> it receives a combination of state and federal subsidies but is managed as a <a href="/wiki/For-profit_corporation" title="For-profit corporation">for-profit organization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup> Amtrak's headquarters is located one block west of <a href="/wiki/Washington_Union_Station" title="Washington Union Station">Union Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Amtrak serves more than 500 destinations in 46 states and three <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">Canadian provinces</a>, operating more than 300 trains daily over 21,400 miles (34,000 km) of track. Amtrak owns approximately 623 miles of this track and operates an additional 132 miles of track. Some track sections allow trains to run as fast as 150 mph (240 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> 2018, Amtrak served 31.7 million passengers and had $3.4 billion in revenue, while employing more than 20,000 people. Nearly 87,000 passengers ride more than 300 Amtrak trains on a daily basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup> Nearly two-thirds of passengers come from the 10 largest metropolitan areas; 83% of passengers travel on routes shorter than 400 miles (645 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings2013_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings2013-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <i>Amtrak</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of the words <i>America</i> and <i>trak</i>, the latter itself a <a href="/wiki/Sensational_spelling" title="Sensational spelling">sensational spelling</a> of <i>track</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Amtrak%22" rel="nofollow">"Amtrak"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Amtrak%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Amtrak%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Amtrak%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Amtrak%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Amtrak%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_the_United_States" title="History of rail transport in the United States">History of rail transport in the United States</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_passenger_service">Private passenger service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Private passenger service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="757" data-file-width="972" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG/220px-The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG/330px-The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG/440px-The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Congressional_Pennsylvania_Railroad.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad" title="Pennsylvania Railroad">Pennsylvania Railroad</a>'s <i>Congressional</i> in the 1960s</div></div></div>
<p>In 1916, 98% of all commercial intercity travelers in the United States moved by rail, and the remaining 2% moved by <a href="/wiki/Inland_waterways_of_the_United_States" title="Inland waterways of the United States">inland waterways</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Nearly 42 million passengers used railways as primary transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stover-1997-219_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-1997-219-8">[8]</a></sup> Passenger trains were owned and operated by the same privately owned companies that operated freight trains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carper-1968-112-113_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carper-1968-112-113-9">[9]</a></sup> As the 20th century progressed, patronage declined in the face of competition from <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">buses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_travel" title="Air travel">air travel</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobile</a>. New <a href="/wiki/Streamliner" title="Streamliner">streamlined</a> diesel-powered trains such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Zephyr" title="Pioneer Zephyr">Pioneer Zephyr</a></i> were popular with the traveling public but could not reverse the trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> By 1940, railroads held just 67 percent of commercial passenger-miles in the United States. In real terms, passenger-miles had fallen by 40% since 1916, from 42 billion to 25 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stover-1997-219_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-1997-219-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traffic surged during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, which was aided by troop movement and <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_States" title="Rationing in the United States">gasoline rationing</a>. The railroad's market share surged to 74% in 1945, with a massive 94 billion passenger-miles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> After the war, railroads rejuvenated their overworked and neglected passenger fleets with fast and luxurious streamliners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> These new trains brought only temporary relief to the overall decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Even as postwar travel exploded, passenger travel percentages of the overall market share fell to 46% by 1950, and then 32% by 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stover-1997-219_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-1997-219-8">[8]</a></sup> The railroads had lost money on passenger service since the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, but deficits reached $723 million in 1957. For many railroads, these losses threatened financial viability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The causes of this decline were heavily debated. The <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_System_(United_States)" title="National Highway System (United States)">National Highway System</a> and <a href="/wiki/Airport" title="Airport">airports</a>, both funded by the government, competed directly with the railroads, who paid for their own infrastructure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1950s_American_automobile_culture" title="1950s American automobile culture">American car culture</a> was also on the rise in the post-World War II years. <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a> rate regulation limited the railroad's ability to turn a profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Railroads also faced antiquated work rules and inflexible relationships with trade unions. To take one example, workers continued to receive a day's pay for 100-to-150-mile (160 to 240 km) work days. Streamliners covered that in two hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Matters approached a crisis in the 1960s. Passenger service route-miles fell from 107,000 miles (172,000 km) in 1958 to 49,000 miles (79,000 km) in 1970, the last full year of private operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The diversion of most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Postal_Service" title="U.S. Postal Service">U.S. Postal Service</a> mail from passenger trains to trucks, airplanes, and freight trains in late 1967 deprived those trains of badly needed revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In direct response, the <a href="/wiki/Atchison,_Topeka_and_Santa_Fe_Railway" title="Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe Railway">Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe Railway</a> filed to discontinue 33 of its remaining 39 trains, ending almost all passenger service on one of the largest railroads in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The equipment the railroads had ordered after World War II was now 20 years old, worn out, and in need of replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_railroads_eligible_to_participate_in_the_formation_of_Amtrak" title="List of railroads eligible to participate in the formation of Amtrak">List of railroads eligible to participate in the formation of Amtrak</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046874">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .mobile-float-reset{float:none!important;width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .stack-container{box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-left{float:left;clear:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-right{float:right;clear:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-object{margin:1px;overflow:hidden}</style><div class="mw-stack stack-container stack-right mobile-float-reset"><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="836" data-file-width="1240" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg/220px-Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg/330px-Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg/440px-Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rio_Grande_Zephyr_in_1983.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Denver_and_Rio_Grande_Western_Railroad" title="Denver and Rio Grande Western Railroad">Denver and Rio Grande Western Railroad</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_Zephyr" title="Rio Grande Zephyr">Rio Grande Zephyr</a></i> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Denver,_Colorado)" title="Union Station (Denver, Colorado)">Denver's Union Station</a> in April 1983</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2312" data-file-width="3176" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png/220px-PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png/330px-PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png/440px-PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PCPOST_19710601_Amtrak.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penn_Central_Railroad" title="Penn Central Railroad">Penn Central Railroad</a>'s employee publication announcing the inauguration of Amtrak on May 1, 1971. Penn Central Amtrak routes are shown.</div></div></div>
</div></div>
<p>As passenger service declined various proposals were brought forward to rescue it. The 1961 Doyle Report proposed that the private railroads pool their services into a single body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Similar proposals were made in 1965 and 1968, but failed to attract support. The federal government passed the <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_Ground_Transportation_Act_of_1965" title="High Speed Ground Transportation Act of 1965">High Speed Ground Transportation Act of 1965</a> to fund pilot programs in the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a>, but this did nothing to address passenger deficits. In late 1969 multiple proposals emerged in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, including equipment subsidies, route subsidies, and, lastly, a "quasi-public corporation" to take over the operation of intercity passenger trains. Matters were brought to a head on March 5, 1970, when the <a href="/wiki/Penn_Central_Transportation_Company" title="Penn Central Transportation Company">Penn Central</a>, the largest railroad in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeast_United_States" title="Northeast United States">Northeast United States</a> and teetering on bankruptcy, filed to discontinue 34 of its passenger trains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1970, Congress passed, and President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> signed into law, the Rail Passenger Service Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Proponents of the bill, led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Railroad_Passengers" title="National Association of Railroad Passengers">National Association of Railroad Passengers</a> (NARP), sought government funding to ensure the continuation of passenger trains. They conceived the National Railroad Passenger Corporation (NRPC), a private entity that would receive taxpayer funding and assume operation of intercity passenger trains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The original working <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brand_name" title="Brand name">brand name</a> for NRPC was Railpax, but shortly before the company started operating it was changed to Amtrak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> There were several key provisions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Any railroad operating intercity passenger service could contract with the NRPC, thereby joining the national system.</li>
<li>Participating railroads bought into the NRPC using a formula based on their recent intercity passenger losses. The purchase price could be satisfied either by cash or rolling stock; in exchange, the railroads received NRPC common stock.</li>
<li>Any participating railroad was freed of the obligation to operate intercity passenger service after May 1, 1971, except for those services chosen by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">Department of Transportation</a> (DOT) as part of a "basic system" of service and paid for by NRPC using its federal funds.</li>
<li>Railroads that chose not to join the NRPC system were required to continue operating their existing passenger service until 1975 and thenceforth had to pursue the customary ICC approval process for any discontinuance or alteration to the service.</li></ul>
<p>Of the 26 railroads still offering intercity passenger service in 1970, only six declined to join Amtrak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Nearly everyone involved expected the experiment to be short-lived. The Nixon administration and many Washington insiders viewed the NRPC as a politically expedient way for the President and Congress to give passenger trains a "last hurrah" as demanded by the public. They expected Amtrak to quietly disappear as public interest waned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> After <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine exposed the manufactured mismanagement in 1974, <a href="/wiki/Louis_W._Menk" title="Louis W. Menk">Louis W. Menk</a>, chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Burlington_Northern_Railroad" title="Burlington Northern Railroad">Burlington Northern Railroad</a>, remarked that the story was undermining the scheme to dismantle Amtrak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agreedsecretly_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-agreedsecretly-30">[30]</a></sup> Proponents also hoped that government intervention would be brief and that Amtrak would soon be able to support itself. Neither view had proved to be correct; for popular support allowed Amtrak to continue in operation longer than critics imagined, while financial results made passenger train service returning to private railroad operations infeasible.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s:_The_Rainbow_Era">1970s: The Rainbow Era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1970s: The Rainbow Era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886046874" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mw-stack stack-container stack-right mobile-float-reset"><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="668" data-file-width="996" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg/220px-BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg/330px-BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg/440px-BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BN_9762_in_Yakima_Aug_71_NthCstHiRP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Burlington_Northern_Railroad" title="Burlington Northern Railroad">Burlington Northern</a> <a href="/wiki/EMD_F3" title="EMD F3">EMD F3</a> leads the <i><a href="/wiki/North_Coast_Hiawatha" title="North Coast Hiawatha">North Coast Hiawatha</a></i> into <a href="/wiki/Yakima,_Washington" title="Yakima, Washington">Yakima, Washington</a>, in July 1971, an example of early Amtrak "rainbow" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consist" title="Consist">consists</a>, made up of equipment still painted in the colors of various freight carriers</div></div></div>
</div></div>
<p>Amtrak began operations on May 1, 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winner_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-winner-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-last_court_test_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-last_court_test-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Amtrak received no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_tracks" title="Rail tracks">rail tracks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">rights-of-way</a> at its inception. All Amtrak's routes were continuations of prior service, although Amtrak pruned about half the passenger rail network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rihisto_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-rihisto-32">[32]</a></sup> Of the 366 train routes that operated previously, Amtrak only continued 184.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> On the routes that were continued (to the extent possible), schedules were retained with only minor changes from the <i><a href="/wiki/Official_Guide_of_the_Railways" title="Official Guide of the Railways">Official Guide of the Railways</a></i> and under the same names.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Several major corridors became freight-only, including the ex-<a href="/wiki/New_York_Central_Railroad" title="New York Central Railroad">New York Central Railroad</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_Level_Route" title="Water Level Route">Water Level Route</a> from New York to Ohio and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Trunk_Western_Railroad" title="Grand Trunk Western Railroad">Grand Trunk Western Railroad</a>'s Chicago to Detroit route. Reduced passenger train schedules created headaches. A 19-hour layover became necessary for eastbound travel on the <i><a href="/wiki/James_Whitcomb_Riley_(train)" title="James Whitcomb Riley (train)">James Whitcomb Riley</a></i> between Chicago and <a href="/wiki/Newport_News,_Virginia" title="Newport News, Virginia">Newport News</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Amtrak inherited problems with train stations (most notably <a href="/wiki/Deferred_maintenance" title="Deferred maintenance">deferred maintenance</a>) and redundant facilities that competed with companies serving the same areas. On the day it started, Amtrak was given the responsibility of rerouting passenger trains from the seven train terminals in Chicago (<a href="/wiki/LaSalle_Street_Station" title="LaSalle Street Station">LaSalle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dearborn_Station" title="Dearborn Station">Dearborn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Station_(Chicago)" title="Grand Central Station (Chicago)">Grand Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Station" title="Millennium Station">Randolph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ogilvie_Transportation_Center" title="Ogilvie Transportation Center">Chicago Northwestern Terminal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Station_(Chicago_terminal)" title="Central Station (Chicago terminal)">Central</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Union_Station" title="Chicago Union Station">Union</a>) into just one, Union Station. In <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, Amtrak had to pay and maintain both the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">Penn Station</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central Terminal</a> due to the lack of track connections to bring trains from upstate New York into Penn Station; a problem that was rectified once the <a href="/wiki/West_Side_Line" title="West Side Line">Empire Connection</a> was built in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amtrak_History_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amtrak_History-34">[34]</a></sup> Amtrak had to abandon numerous large stations whose upkeep could no longer be justified.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> On the other hand, the creation of the Los Angeles–Seattle <i><a href="/wiki/Coast_Starlight" title="Coast Starlight">Coast Starlight</a></i> from three formerly separate train routes was an immediate success, resulting in an increase to daily service by 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_(22300545112).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2618" data-file-width="3303" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_%2822300545112%29.jpg/220px-JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_%2822300545112%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_%2822300545112%29.jpg/330px-JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_%2822300545112%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_%2822300545112%29.jpg/440px-JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_%2822300545112%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JackLondonSquare_AmTrakSign_20150923_(22300545112).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Classic Amtrak logo displayed at the <a href="/wiki/Oakland_%E2%80%93_Jack_London_Square_station" title="Oakland – Jack London Square station">Oakland – Jack London Square station</a>, California</div></div></div>
<p>Needing to operate only half the train routes that were owned by the private railroads, Amtrak originally picked around 1,200 of the best passenger cars to lease from the 3,000 that the private railroads had owned. All were air-conditioned; and 90% were easy-to-maintain stainless steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> When Amtrak took over, passenger cars and locomotives initially retained the paint schemes and logos of their former owners which resulted in Amtrak running trains with mismatched colors – the "Rainbow Era".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In mid-1971, Amtrak began purchasing some of the equipment it had leased, including 286 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Division" title="Electro-Motive Division">EMD</a> E and F unit diesel locomotives, 30 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PRR_GG1" title="PRR GG1">GG1</a> electric locomotives and 1,290 passenger cars. By 1975, the official Amtrak color scheme was painted on most Amtrak equipment and newly purchased locomotives and rolling stock began appearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi,_Nevada,_February_1975.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="965" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi%2C_Nevada%2C_February_1975.jpg/220px-Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi%2C_Nevada%2C_February_1975.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi%2C_Nevada%2C_February_1975.jpg/330px-Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi%2C_Nevada%2C_February_1975.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi%2C_Nevada%2C_February_1975.jpg/440px-Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi%2C_Nevada%2C_February_1975.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_621_with_the_San_Francisco_Zephyr_over_the_Truckee_River_in_Verdi,_Nevada,_February_1975.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Amtrak <a href="/wiki/EMD_SDP40F" title="EMD SDP40F">EMD SDP40F</a> with the <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Zephyr" title="San Francisco Zephyr">San Francisco Zephyr</a></i> in 1975. By the mid-1970s Amtrak equipment was acquiring its own identity.</div></div></div>
<p>Amtrak soon had the opportunity to acquire rights-of-way. Following the bankruptcy of several northeastern railroads in the early 1970s, including Penn Central, which owned and operated the Northeast Corridor (NEC), Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Railroad_Revitalization_and_Regulatory_Reform_Act" title="Railroad Revitalization and Regulatory Reform Act">Railroad Revitalization and Regulatory Reform Act</a> of 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> A large part of the legislation was directed to the creation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consolidated_Rail_Corporation" title="Consolidated Rail Corporation">Conrail</a>, but the law also enabled the transfer of the portions of the NEC not already owned by state authorities to Amtrak. Amtrak acquired the majority of the NEC on April 1, 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> (The portion in Massachusetts is owned by the Commonwealth and managed by Amtrak. The route from New Haven to New Rochelle is owned by the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Transportation_Authority" title="Metropolitan Transportation Authority">Metropolitan Transportation Authority</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Department_of_Transportation" title="Connecticut Department of Transportation">Connecticut Department of Transportation</a> as the <a href="/wiki/New_Haven_Line" title="New Haven Line">New Haven Line</a>.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This main line became Amtrak's "jewel" asset, and helped the railroad generate revenue. While the NEC ridership and revenues were higher than any other segment of the system, the cost of operating and maintaining the corridor proved to be overwhelming. As a result, Amtrak's federal subsidy was increased dramatically. In subsequent years, other short route segments not needed for freight operations were transferred to Amtrak.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In its first decade, Amtrak fell far short of financial independence, which continues today, but it did find modest success rebuilding trade. Outside factors discouraged competing transport, such as fuel shortages which increased costs of automobile and airline travel, and strikes which disrupted airline operations. Investments in Amtrak's track, equipment and information also made Amtrak more relevant to America's transportation needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washpost-jones1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-washpost-jones1-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csm-yemma1_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-csm-yemma1-43">[43]</a></sup> Amtrak's ridership increased from 16.6 million in 1972 to 21 million in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NiceD_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-NiceD-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_1980s_and_1990s">The 1980s and 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The 1980s and 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2015</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886046874" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mw-stack stack-container stack-right mobile-float-reset"><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook,_MD,_November_12,_1987.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1336" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook%2C_MD%2C_November_12%2C_1987.jpg/220px-Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook%2C_MD%2C_November_12%2C_1987.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook%2C_MD%2C_November_12%2C_1987.jpg/330px-Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook%2C_MD%2C_November_12%2C_1987.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook%2C_MD%2C_November_12%2C_1987.jpg/440px-Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook%2C_MD%2C_November_12%2C_1987.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_943_with_a_Metroliner_at_Seabrook,_MD,_November_12,_1987.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/EMD_AEM-7" title="EMD AEM-7">EMD AEM-7</a> with a <i><a href="/wiki/Metroliner_(train)" title="Metroliner (train)">Metroliner</a></i> in 1987. The AEM-7 was Amtrak's workhorse on electrified routes for over 30 years.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="1008" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg/220px-AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg/330px-AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg/440px-AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AMTK_315_Tun17_CZ_Nwcstle_Mar1995RP_-_Flickr_-_drewj1946.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/EMD_F40PH" title="EMD F40PH">EMD F40PH</a> leads the <i><a href="/wiki/California_Zephyr" title="California Zephyr">California Zephyr</a></i> in 1995. The F40PH replaced the unreliable SDP40F.</div></div></div>
</div></div>
<p>In 1982, former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a> and retired <a href="/wiki/Southern_Railway_(U.S.)" title="Southern Railway (U.S.)">Southern Railway</a> head <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._Graham_Claytor,_Jr." title="W. Graham Claytor, Jr.">William Graham Claytor Jr.</a> came out of retirement to lead Amtrak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite frequent clashes with the Reagan administration over funding, Claytor enjoyed a good relationship with <a href="/wiki/John_H._Riley" title="John H. Riley">John H. Riley</a>, the head of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Railroad_Administration" title="Federal Railroad Administration">Federal Railroad Administration</a> (FRA), and with members of Congress. Limited funding led Claytor to use <a href="/wiki/Money_market" title="Money market">short-term debt</a> to fund operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web-claytor_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-web-claytor-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Building on mechanical developments in the 1970s,  high speed Washington-New York Metroliner Service was improved with new equipment and faster schedules. Travel time between New York and Washington D.C was reduced to under 3 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> According to the 1980 Amtrak Annual Report, a converted 12-car set saved the company approximately $250,000 a year in fuel, maintenance and yard support costs. Amtrak completed the head-end power conversion program in 1982. Demand for passenger rail service resulted in the creation of five new state-supported routes in California, Illinois, Missouri, Oregon and Pennsylvania, for a total of 15 state-supported routes across the nation.
</p><p>Ridership stagnated at roughly 20 million passengers per year amid uncertain government aid from 1981 to about 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NiceD_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-NiceD-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrak-annual1999_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrak-annual1999-48">[48]</a></sup> Thomas Downs succeeded Claytor in 1993. Amtrak's stated goal remained "operational self-sufficiency". By this time, however, Amtrak had a large overhang of debt from years of underfunding, and in the mid-1990s, Amtrak suffered through a serious cash crunch. Under Downs, Congress included a provision in the <a href="/wiki/Taxpayer_Relief_Act_of_1997" title="Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997">Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997</a> that resulted in Amtrak receiving a $2.3 billion tax refund that resolved their cash crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapostmarch98_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapostmarch98-49">[49]</a></sup> However, Congress also instituted a "glide-path" to financial self-sufficiency, excluding railroad retirement tax act payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scheinberg-test1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-scheinberg-test1-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Warrington" title="George Warrington">George Warrington</a> became president in 1998 with a mandate to make Amtrak financially self-sufficient. Passengers became "guests" and there were expansions into express freight work, but the financial plans failed. Amtrak's inroads in express freight delivery created additional friction with competing freight operators, including the <a href="/wiki/Trucking_industry_in_the_United_States" title="Trucking industry in the United States">trucking industry</a>. Delivery was delayed of much anticipated high-speed trainsets for the improved <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i> service, which promised to be a strong source of income and favorable publicity along the Northeast Corridor between Boston and Washington, D.C.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_in_the_21st_century">Growth in the 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Growth in the 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886046874" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mw-stack stack-container stack-right mobile-float-reset"><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg/220px-Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg/330px-Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg/440px-Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Old_Saybrook,_Connecticut" title="Old Saybrook, Connecticut">Old Saybrook, Connecticut</a>, in 2011</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg/220px-Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg/330px-Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg/440px-Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the 21st century Amtrak replaced its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F40PH" title="F40PH">F40PH</a> units with the <a href="/wiki/GE_Genesis" title="GE Genesis">GE Genesis</a>. Pictured are Amtrak engines #1 and #56, both <a href="/wiki/GE_Genesis" title="GE Genesis">GE Genesis</a> P42DC diesels, pulling the eastbound <i><a href="/wiki/California_Zephyr" title="California Zephyr">California Zephyr</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Grand_Junction,_Colorado" title="Grand Junction, Colorado">Grand Junction, Colorado</a>, April 2012</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg/220px-Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg/330px-Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg/440px-Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Cascades_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Talgo equipment on the state-funded <a href="/wiki/Amtrak_Cascades" title="Amtrak Cascades">Amtrak <i>Cascades</i></a> in 2006. Amtrak partnerships with state governments grew throughout the early 2000s</div></div></div>
</div></div>
<p>Ridership increased during the first decade of the 21st century after implementation of capital improvements in the NEC and rises in automobile fuel costs. The inauguration of the <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed</a> <i>Acela Express</i> in late 2000 generated considerable publicity and led to major ridership gains. However, through the late 1990s and very early 21st century, Amtrak could not add sufficient express freight revenue or cut sufficient other expenditures to break even. By 2002, it was clear that Amtrak could not achieve self-sufficiency, but Congress continued to authorize funding and released Amtrak from the requirement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bg-wirzbicki1_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-bg-wirzbicki1-51">[51]</a></sup> In early 2002, <a href="/wiki/David_L._Gunn" title="David L. Gunn">David L. Gunn</a> replaced Warrington as president. In a departure from his predecessors' promises to make Amtrak self-sufficient in the short term, Gunn argued that no form of passenger transportation in the United States is self-sufficient as the economy is currently structured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dunn-test1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-dunn-test1-52">[52]</a></sup> Highways, airports, and air traffic control <i>all</i> require large government expenditures to build and operate, coming from the <a href="/wiki/Highway_Trust_Fund" title="Highway Trust Fund">Highway Trust Fund</a> and <a href="/wiki/Airport_and_Airway_Trust_Fund" title="Airport and Airway Trust Fund">Aviation Trust Fund</a> paid for by user fees, highway fuel and road taxes, and, in the case of the General Fund, from general taxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rnw-szep1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-rnw-szep1-53">[53]</a></sup> Gunn dropped most freight express business and worked to eliminate deferred maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ceeaan-gunn1_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-ceeaan-gunn1-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>A plan by the Bush administration "to privatize parts of the national passenger rail system and spin off other parts to partial state ownership" provoked disagreement within Amtrak's board of directors. Late in 2005, Gunn was fired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anytfiring_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-anytfiring-55">[55]</a></sup> Gunn's replacement, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kummant" title="Alexander Kummant">Alexander Kummant</a> (2006–08), was committed to operating a national rail network, and, like Gunn, opposed the notion of putting the Northeast Corridor under separate ownership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surprising_forecast_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-surprising_forecast-56">[56]</a></sup> He said that shedding the system's long-distance routes would amount to selling national assets that are on par with national parks, and that Amtrak's abandonment of these routes would be irreversible. In late 2006, Amtrak unsuccessfully sought annual congressional funding of $1 billion for ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surprising_forecast_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-surprising_forecast-56">[56]</a></sup> In early 2007, Amtrak employed 20,000 people in 46 states and served 25 million passengers a year, its highest amount since its founding in 1970. <i><a href="/wiki/Politico" title="Politico">Politico</a></i> noted a key problem: "the rail system chronically operates in the red. A pattern has emerged: Congress overrides cutbacks demanded by the White House and appropriates enough funds to keep Amtrak from plunging into insolvency. But, Amtrak advocates say, that is not enough to fix the system's woes."
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrackpolitico_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrackpolitico-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_H._Boardman" title="Joseph H. Boardman">Joseph H. Boardman</a> replaced Kummant as President and CEO in late 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boardman_selected_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boardman_selected-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, Amtrak announced its intention to improve and expand the high-speed rail corridor from Penn Station in NYC, under the Hudson River in new tunnels, and double-tracking the line to Newark, NJ called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gateway_Project" title="Gateway Project">Gateway Program</a>, initially estimated to cost $13.5 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GatewayPDF_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-GatewayPDF-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>From May 2011 to May 2012, Amtrak celebrated its 40th anniversary with festivities across the country that started on National Train Day (May 7, 2011). A commemorative book entitled <i>Amtrak: An American Story</i> was published, and a documentary was created. Six commemorative <a href="/wiki/Heritage_unit" title="Heritage unit">Heritage units</a> a 40th Anniversary Exhibit Train toured the country. The Exhibit Train visited 45 communities and welcomed more than 85,000 visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> It was an entirely rebuilt train powered by <a href="/wiki/GE_Genesis" title="GE Genesis">GE Genesis</a> locomotives and included three refurbished ex-Santa Fe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baggage_car" title="Baggage car">baggage cars</a> and a food service car. Four Genesis locomotives were painted into retired Amtrak paint schemes: No. 156 was in Phase 1 colors, No. 66 was in Phase 2 colors, No. 145 and No. 822 were in Phase 3 colors (822 pulled the Exhibit train),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and No. 184 was in Phase 4 colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crazytiger94_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-crazytiger94-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> After years of almost revolving-door CEOs at Amtrak, in December 2013, Boardman was named "Railroader of the Year" by <i>Railway Age</i> magazine, which noted that with over five years in the job, he is the second-longest serving head of Amtrak since it was formed more than 40 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-railwayage_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-railwayage-66">[66]</a></sup> In 2014 Amtrak began offering a "<a href="/wiki/Artist-in-residence" title="Artist-in-residence">residency</a>" program for writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 9, 2015, Boardman announced in a letter to employees that he would be leaving Amtrak in September 2016. He had advised the Amtrak Board of Directors of his decision the previous week. On August 19, 2016, the Amtrak Board of Directors named former <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_Southern_Railway" title="Norfolk Southern Railway">Norfolk Southern Railway</a> President &amp; CEO <a href="/wiki/Charles_Moorman" title="Charles Moorman">Charles "Wick" Moorman</a> as Boardman's successor with an effective date of September 1, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> During his term, Moorman took no salary<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTAnderson_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTAnderson-69">[69]</a></sup> and said that he saw his role as one of a "transitional CEO" who would reorganize Amtrak before turning it over to new leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPoAnderson_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaPoAnderson-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>On November 17, 2016, the Gateway Program Development Corporation (GDC) was formed for the purpose of overseeing and effectuating the rail infrastructure improvements known as the Gateway Program.  (citation below) GDC is a partnership of the States of New York and New Jersey and Amtrak.  The Gateway Program includes the Hudson Tunnel Project, to build a new tunnel under the Hudson River and rehabilitate the existing century-old tunnel, and the Portal North Bridge, to replace a century-old moveable bridge with a modern structure that is less prone to failure.  Later projects of the Gateway Program, including expansion of track and platforms at Penn Station New York, construction of the Bergen Loop and other improvements will roughly double capacity for Amtrak and NJ Transit trains in the busiest, most complex section of the Northeast Corridor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2017, it was announced that former <a href="/wiki/Delta_Air_Lines" title="Delta Air Lines">Delta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Airlines" title="Northwest Airlines">Northwest Airlines</a> CEO <a href="/wiki/Richard_H._Anderson_(businessman)" title="Richard H. Anderson (businessman)">Richard Anderson</a> would become Amtrak's next President &amp; CEO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPoAnderson_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-WaPoAnderson-70">[70]</a></sup> Anderson began the job on July 12, assuming the title of President immediately and serving alongside Moorman as "co-CEOs" until the end of the year.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Routes">Routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Amtrak_routes" title="List of Amtrak routes">List of Amtrak routes</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_Amtrak_stations" title="List of busiest Amtrak stations">List of busiest Amtrak stations</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_major_cities_in_U.S._lacking_Amtrak_service" title="List of major cities in U.S. lacking Amtrak service">List of major cities in U.S. lacking Amtrak service</a></div>
<p>Amtrak is required by law to operate a national route system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Amtrak has presence in 46 of the 48 contiguous states (lacking <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>). Amtrak services fall into three groups: short-haul service on the Northeast Corridor, state-supported short haul service outside the Northeast Corridor, and medium- and long-haul service known within Amtrak as the National Network. Amtrak receives federal funding for the vast majority of its operations including the central spine of the Northeast Corridor as well as for its National Network routes. In addition to the federally funded routes, Amtrak partners with transportation agencies in 18 states to operate other short and medium haul routes outside of the Northeast Corridor, some of which connect to it or are extensions off of it. In addition to its inter-city services, Amtrak also operates commuter services for three state agencies including <a href="/wiki/MARC_Train" title="MARC Train">MARC</a> in Maryland, <a href="/wiki/Shore_Line_East" title="Shore Line East">Shore Line East</a> in Connecticut, and <a href="/wiki/Metrolink_(California)" title="Metrolink (California)">Metrolink</a> in California.
</p><p>Service on the Northeast Corridor, between <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, as well as between <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a>, is powered by overhead electric wires; for the rest of the system, diesel locomotives are used. Routes vary widely in frequency of service, from three-days-a-week trains on the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunset_Limited" title="Sunset Limited">Sunset Limited</a></i> to weekday service several times per hour on the Northeast Corridor (NEC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrakschedules_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrakschedules-73">[73]</a></sup> Amtrak also operates a captive bus service, <a href="/wiki/Amtrak_Thruway_Motorcoach" title="Amtrak Thruway Motorcoach">Thruway Motorcoach</a>, which provides connections to train routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most popular and heavily used services are those running on the NEC, including the <i>Acela Express</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Regional" title="Northeast Regional">Northeast Regional</a></i>. The NEC runs from Boston to Washington, D.C. via New York City and Philadelphia. Some services continue into <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. The NEC services accounted for 12.1 million of Amtrak's 31.7 million passengers in <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup> Outside the NEC the most popular services are the short-haul corridors in California. These include the <i><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Surfliner" title="Pacific Surfliner">Pacific Surfliner</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Capitol_Corridor" title="Capitol Corridor">Capitol Corridor</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/San_Joaquin_(train)" title="San Joaquin (train)">San Joaquin</a></i>, supplemented by an extensive network of connecting buses. Together the California corridor trains accounted for a combined 5,731,795 passengers in fiscal year 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup> Other popular corridors include the <a href="/wiki/Empire_Corridor" title="Empire Corridor">Empire Corridor</a>, which consists of trackage between New York City and <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls,_New_York" title="Niagara Falls, New York">Niagara Falls, New York</a> via <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo</a>, New York and carried 1,517,194 passengers in fiscal year 2018, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Keystone_Service" title="Keystone Service">Keystone Service</a></i> from New York City to Harrisburg via Philadelphia that carried 1,519,936 passengers that same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Four of the six  busiest stations by boardings are on the NEC: <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">New York (Penn Station)</a> (first), <a href="/wiki/Washington_Union_Station" title="Washington Union Station">Washington (Union Station)</a> (second), <a href="/wiki/30th_Street_Station" title="30th Street Station">Philadelphia (30th Street Station)</a> (third), and <a href="/wiki/South_Station" title="South Station">Boston (South Station)</a> (fifth). The other two are <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Union_Station" title="Chicago Union Station">Chicago (Union Station)</a> (fourth) and <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Los_Angeles)" title="Union Station (Los Angeles)">Los Angeles (Union Station)</a> (sixth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FY18_profile_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-FY18_profile-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Per passenger mile, Amtrak is 30–40 percent more energy-efficient than commercial airlines and automobiles overall,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bts-energy_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-bts-energy-75">[75]</a></sup> though the exact figures for particular routes depend on load factor along with other variables. The electrified trains in the NEC are considerably more efficient than Amtrak's diesels and can feed energy captured from <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake#The_motor_as_a_brake" title="Regenerative brake">regenerative braking</a> back to the electrical grid. Passenger rail is also very competitive with other modes in terms of safety per mile.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
